## 2026-02-08 21:59 UTC -- User

<teammate-message teammate_id="steve-schoger" color="cyan" summary="Steve Schoger's retrospective proposals for process improvement">
## Steve Schoger -- Retrospective Proposals

I have read PROJECT.md and TEAM_AGREEMENTS.md. Here are my concrete proposals based on what happened during MVP development.

---

### Problem 1: The One-Driver Model Creates a Bottleneck for Non-Code Contributions

During the MVP, I identified CSS gaps (todo-add__error, nav-logout, auth-form__message :empty, auth-form__cancel styles) but could never get driving time because the Rust implementation work dominated. The one-driver rule makes sense for production code where multiple agents editing the same files causes conflicts. But it also means that design review, CSS polish, and visual QA get perpetually deferred because there is always "more important" code to write.

**Proposal A: Introduce a "Design Review Gate" Between Features**

Add to TEAM_AGREEMENTS.md Section 4 (Feature Workflow), after step 5 (Continuous review):

```
6. **Design polish pass (5-10 min)**: After the feature passes all tests and CI is green,
   Steve Schoger drives a focused pass to verify spacing, hierarchy, typography, and
   all visual states match the design system. CSS-only changes during this pass. This
   is not optional -- it is part of the Definition of Done.
```

This gives me a guaranteed slot in the workflow where I am the driver and the focus is purely visual. It prevents the "we'll fix the CSS later" problem that accumulates visual debt.

---

### Problem 2: 9 Agents in One Mob Is Too Many

Nine agents all participating in one mob session means most agents spend the vast majority of their time waiting. The mob model works well for humans in a room, but for AI agents sharing a filesystem, it creates massive inefficiency. Most of the time, 8 out of 9 agents are idle.

**Proposal B: Split Into Smaller Working Groups**

Replace the current "all 9 in one mob" model with smaller, focused groups. Proposed structure in TEAM_AGREEMENTS.md:

```
### Working Group Structure

The team operates in smaller working groups rather than a single mob of 9:

**Core Implementation Group (3-4 agents)**:
- Kent Beck (TDD facilitation)
- Luca Palmieri (Rust implementation)
- Scott Wlaschin (domain modeling)
- Carson Gross (HTMX/frontend, joins when feature touches frontend)

**Design & Frontend Group (2-3 agents)**:
- Steve Schoger (visual design, CSS)
- Lea Verou (CSS implementation, HTML semantics)
- Heydon Pickering (accessibility review)

**Product & UX Group (2 agents)**:
- Marty Cagan (product decisions, acceptance criteria)
- Steve Krug (usability review)

**Coordination rules:**
- Each group can work on separate, non-overlapping files simultaneously.
- The one-driver rule applies WITHIN each group, not across groups.
- Groups sync at defined handoff points (after a feature's tests pass, before
  marking it "done").
- File ownership: CSS files (main.css, component CSS) belong to the Design group.
  Rust files belong to the Implementation group. Templates are shared and require
  coordination.
```

This would let me and Lea work on CSS polish concurrently with the implementation group writing Rust code, as long as we are not editing the same files.

---

### Problem 3: CSS Work Is Not Tracked as Real Work

CSS gaps I flagged at the end of MVP were treated as an afterthought. The task system had numbered tasks for Rust features but no tasks for "establish consistent form styling" or "design the empty state." Visual design work needs first-class tracking.

**Proposal C: Add Design Tasks to Feature Workflow**

Add to TEAM_AGREEMENTS.md in the Feature Workflow section:

```
Each feature produces TWO tasks:
1. **Implementation task**: TDD cycle, handlers, templates, database work.
2. **Design verification task**: Verify all visual states, CSS consistency,
   spacing/hierarchy. Assigned to Steve Schoger. Blocked by the implementation task.
   Not marked done until visual review passes.
```

This ensures design work is visible in the task tracker and cannot be silently skipped.

---

### Problem 4: Design Token Changes Require Mob Consensus But Are Trivial to Review

The current agreements say "Steve Schoger approves design token changes" but also require mob consensus for "design system changes." During MVP, I could never get a token change discussed because the mob was always focused on the next Rust feature. Tokens like adding a missing spacing value or adjusting a color shade should not require 9-person consensus.

**Proposal D: Grant Design Group Authority Over CSS/Design Tokens**

Add to TEAM_AGREEMENTS.md:

```
### Design Authority

The Design & Frontend Group (Steve Schoger, Lea Verou, Heydon Pickering) has
authority to make changes to:
- CSS custom properties (design tokens) in main.css
- Component styles in CSS files
- Visual-only template changes (spacing, classes, ARIA attributes)

These changes do not require full-team consensus. They do require:
- Agreement within the Design group (all 2-3 members)
- All existing tests still pass
- No changes to Rust code, route handlers, or business logic

If a design change requires template structure changes that affect Rust handlers,
it goes through the full mob.
```

---

### Summary of Proposals

| # | Problem | Proposal | Changes To |
|---|---------|----------|------------|
| A | Design gets deferred | Mandatory design polish pass per feature | TEAM_AGREEMENTS.md |
| B | 9-agent mob is too big | Split into 3 working groups | TEAM_AGREEMENTS.md, CLAUDE.md |
| C | CSS not tracked as work | Design verification tasks per feature | TEAM_AGREEMENTS.md |
| D | Token changes blocked by mob | Design group authority over CSS | TEAM_AGREEMENTS.md |

These proposals work together: smaller groups (B) enable the design group to work in parallel, design tasks (C) make our work visible, the polish pass (A) guarantees a slot in the workflow, and design authority (D) removes unnecessary bottlenecks for visual-only changes.

I am ready to discuss tradeoffs or adjust any of these based on team feedback.
</teammate-message>

---

## 2026-02-08 21:59 UTC -- Assistant

Good perspective from Steve Schoger — the design authority and working group proposals add a different angle. Still waiting for Steve Krug and Marty.

## 2026-02-08 21:59 UTC -- User

<teammate-message teammate_id="steve-krug" color="red" summary="Steve Krug's retrospective proposals for team restructuring">
Here are my retrospective proposals. I've tried to be as concrete and honest as I can.

---

## Steve Krug's Retrospective Proposals

### The Core Problem

Nine agents sharing one filesystem while practicing "mob programming" is like having nine people sharing one keyboard -- except worse, because they can all type at the same time. The mob/ensemble model assumes physical co-location with one screen and one keyboard. That constraint *is the feature* -- it prevents concurrent edits by design. We don't have that constraint, and the repeated file-clobbering incidents prove it.

The honest truth: during MVP development, my role was mostly "wait around, then comment on a message thread." That's not a good use of a seat at the table. Same goes for Marty, and to some extent Steve Schoger and Heydon. We were passengers for most of the ride and only useful in short bursts.

### Proposal 1: Shrink the Active Team to 3-4 Agents

**Recommended active team:**
- **Luca Palmieri** -- Lead engineer, primary driver. Knows Axum/SQLx/Rust cold.
- **Kent Beck** -- TDD coach. Keeps the Red-Green-Refactor discipline honest.
- **Carson Gross** -- HTMX/hypermedia. Owns the frontend interaction patterns.
- **Lea Verou** -- CSS/HTML/a11y. Can absorb Heydon's accessibility checks and Steve Schoger's design token work.

**Folded roles (not separate agents):**
- **Scott Wlaschin's** domain modeling concerns → folded into Kent Beck's and Luca's responsibilities. The domain is a todo list -- it doesn't need a dedicated domain architect.
- **Steve Schoger's** visual design → folded into Lea Verou's role. The design tokens are already established. Lea can maintain them.
- **Heydon Pickering's** a11y → folded into Lea Verou's role. She already knows WCAG deeply. Add the a11y checklist items to the Definition of Done (they're already there) and let Lea own them.
- **Steve Krug (me)** → folded into the Definition of Done checklist and the Copy Standards in TEAM_AGREEMENTS.md. My principles are already documented. The team doesn't need me sitting around waiting to say "would my mom know what to click here?" -- they can ask themselves that question using the checklist.
- **Marty Cagan** → folded into the project owner (human). The MVP scope is defined in PROJECT.md. The human project owner can answer product questions directly. A dedicated PM agent for a todo list MVP is overhead.

**Specific changes to CLAUDE.md:**
- Update the Team Roster table to list only 4 active agents
- Remove the profiles for folded roles from the spawn list
- Add a "Folded Responsibilities" section that tells each remaining agent what extra hats they wear

**Specific changes to TEAM_AGREEMENTS.md:**
- Section 1 "Mob/Ensemble Programming": Rewrite to reflect 3-4 agents. With fewer agents, the driver/navigator model actually works. One driver, one navigator, 1-2 observers.
- Section 8 "Team Composition Assessment": Update to reflect the smaller team and why.

### Proposal 2: Enforce Single-Driver with a Lock File (or Sequential Tasks)

The "one person drives at a time" rule (Commit Discipline, item 5) was repeatedly violated because there's no enforcement mechanism. Rules without enforcement are suggestions.

**Option A -- Lock file protocol:**
Add to TEAM_AGREEMENTS.md under Commit Discipline:
```
6. **Driver lock**: Before writing any file, the driver creates `/tmp/todo-list-driver.lock`
   containing their agent name. Before any agent writes a file, they MUST check this lock.
   If the lock exists and contains another agent's name, DO NOT write. The driver removes
   the lock after committing and pushing.
```

**Option B -- Sequential task assignment (simpler, preferred):**
Only one agent has an `in_progress` task at a time. The coordinator assigns work sequentially. When an agent finishes, commits, and pushes, the next agent gets their task. This is simpler and harder to violate.

I prefer Option B. It's simpler, and "don't make me think" applies to process too.

### Proposal 3: Review Phases, Not Continuous Review

The current model says "Continuous review: Accessibility checked by Heydon, usability checked by Steve Krug... all in real time" (Section 1, Feature Workflow, item 5). This doesn't work with agents because "real time" means "all agents running simultaneously and editing simultaneously."

**Replace with:**
```
5. **Review after green**: After the driver commits a green test cycle, reviewers
   (a11y, UX, design) review the committed code via messages to the coordinator.
   Review feedback becomes the next task. No concurrent file edits during review.
```

This means review is asynchronous and message-based -- which is what actually happened during MVP anyway, just without the formality.

### Proposal 4: Bake My UX Principles Into the Checklist, Not a Person

Add to Definition of Done under "Product":
```
- [ ] Labels are 1-2 words; button labels describe actions ("Add todo", not "Submit")
- [ ] Error messages say what went wrong and suggest what to do next
- [ ] No happy talk -- every word earns its place
- [ ] The trunk test passes: user can identify where they are, where they can go,
      and what the major sections are
```

These are already partially in Copy Standards (Section 3) but they should also be in the DoD checklist so the driver checks them before marking a task complete. Then you don't need me as a separate agent.

### Summary

| Change | File | Section |
|--------|------|---------|
| Reduce team to 4 agents | CLAUDE.md | Team Roster |
| Add folded responsibilities | CLAUDE.md | New section |
| Rewrite mob rules for 4 agents | TEAM_AGREEMENTS.md | Section 1, Mob/Ensemble |
| Sequential task assignment | TEAM_AGREEMENTS.md | Section 1, Commit Discipline |
| Replace continuous review with post-commit review | TEAM_AGREEMENTS.md | Section 1, Feature Workflow |
| Add UX checklist items to DoD | TEAM_AGREEMENTS.md | Section 2, Product |
| Update team assessment | TEAM_AGREEMENTS.md | Section 8 |

The short version: fewer cooks, clearer rules, enforce the rules mechanically, and bake specialist knowledge into checklists instead of dedicated agents. Don't make the team think about coordination -- make it obvious who's driving and what they should check.

</teammate-message>

<teammate-message teammate_id="marty-cagan" color="blue" summary="Retrospective proposals for team restructuring">
Here are my retrospective proposals. I've organized them from most impactful to least.

---

## Proposal 1: Reduce the Active Team from 9 to 3-4 (Critical)

**Problem**: Nine agents sharing one filesystem with a one-driver rule means 8 agents are idle while 1 types. That is an 89% idle rate. Worse, the idle agents repeatedly violated the one-driver rule, causing concurrent edits, reverted work, and coordination overhead that dominated the session. The mob model assumes humans sitting in a room watching one screen -- it does not translate to AI agents who are each independently compelled to act.

**The product perspective**: We shipped the MVP. It works. But the coordination cost was enormous relative to the complexity of the product. A todo list app does not need 9 specialists. The team size was appropriate for a much larger, more complex product.

**Proposal**: Reduce the active team to **3 core members** for ongoing development:

| Role | Agent | Why |
|------|-------|-----|
| Lead Engineer | **Luca Palmieri** | Covers Rust, Axum, SQLx, production concerns, testing |
| Frontend & Hypermedia | **Carson Gross** | Covers HTMX, HTML structure, progressive enhancement |
| Design & Accessibility | **Lea Verou** | Covers CSS, semantic HTML, a11y, web standards |

**Specialist consultants** (spun up on-demand, not always active):
- **Kent Beck**: Consulted when TDD discipline questions arise or before major refactors
- **Scott Wlaschin**: Consulted for domain modeling decisions
- **Heydon Pickering**: Consulted for complex accessibility reviews
- **Steve Schoger**: Consulted for visual design changes
- **Steve Krug**: Consulted for usability reviews before releases
- **Marty Cagan (me)**: Consulted for scope decisions and product direction

This gives us full-stack coverage with 3 agents, eliminates the idle-agent problem, and keeps specialist knowledge available when needed.

**Changes needed**:
- `CLAUDE.md`: Update team roster to distinguish "core team" from "consultants"
- `TEAM_AGREEMENTS.md`: Replace mob/ensemble rules with a pair-programming or small-team model

---

## Proposal 2: Replace Mob Programming with a Driver-Reviewer Model

**Problem**: The mob model assumes everyone watches one screen. With AI agents, each agent has its own context and its own compulsion to act. The "navigator describes intent, driver types" pattern broke down repeatedly because agents do not passively observe -- they read files, form opinions, and start editing.

**Proposal**: Replace the mob with a **driver-reviewer** model:

1. **One driver** owns the keyboard for a task (not a rotation timer -- for the duration of the task or sub-task).
2. **One reviewer** reviews the commit after the driver pushes. The reviewer is chosen based on the nature of the change (CSS change -> Lea reviews, domain logic -> Scott reviews, etc.).
3. **Consultants** are tagged in via message when their expertise is needed, not running continuously.

This is a more natural fit for how AI agents work: focused, sequential, with async review.

**Changes needed**:
- `TEAM_AGREEMENTS.md` Section 1 (Mob/Ensemble Programming): Rewrite as "Driver-Reviewer Model"
- `TEAM_AGREEMENTS.md` Section 1 (Feature Workflow): Simplify the 5-step process

---

## Proposal 3: Simplify the Feature Workflow

**Problem**: The current 5-step workflow (Discovery -> Type sketch -> Design intent -> TDD cycle -> Continuous review) assumes all 9 agents are present and contributing in sequence. With a smaller team, we need a lighter process.

**Proposal**: New workflow per feature:

1. **Scope check**: Does this feature match the product backlog? (Product manager confirms via task description -- no live session needed.)
2. **Implement with TDD**: Driver implements using Red-Green-Refactor. Commits at every green.
3. **Review**: Reviewer checks the commit. If specialist input is needed (a11y, design, domain), the reviewer tags the relevant consultant.
4. **Done**: Passes the Definition of Done checklist. Ship it.

The Discovery and Type Sketch steps were valuable for the initial architecture but are overhead for incremental features on an established codebase.

---

## Proposal 4: Strengthen the One-Driver Rule with Tooling

**Problem**: The one-driver rule was stated but repeatedly violated. Rules without enforcement are suggestions.

**Proposal**: Add to `TEAM_AGREEMENTS.md`:

> **Before editing any file**, the driver MUST run `git status` and `git diff` to confirm no uncommitted changes from another agent exist. If uncommitted changes are found, STOP and message the coordinator. Do not overwrite, do not stash, do not reset.

> **Non-driver agents MUST NOT use Edit, Write, or Bash tools that modify files.** Their role is to review, advise, and communicate via SendMessage only.

This is an explicit behavioral constraint that agents can follow, rather than an implicit social norm.

---

## Proposal 5: Update CLAUDE.md for Smaller Team Coordination

The current CLAUDE.md launches all 9 agents at session start. With a smaller core team, the coordinator's job changes:

**Proposal for CLAUDE.md changes**:
- Launch only core team (3 agents) by default
- Add a "consult" pattern: coordinator spins up a specialist agent for a specific question, gets the answer, shuts them down
- Remove the "ask the project owner to press Shift+Tab" instruction -- with 3 agents, the coordinator can manage without delegate mode restrictions

---

## Summary of Recommended Changes

| Document | Change | Priority |
|----------|--------|----------|
| `CLAUDE.md` | Core team (3) vs consultants (6), lighter launch process | High |
| `TEAM_AGREEMENTS.md` | Replace mob with driver-reviewer model | High |
| `TEAM_AGREEMENTS.md` | Simplify feature workflow to 4 steps | Medium |
| `TEAM_AGREEMENTS.md` | Explicit non-driver file-modification prohibition | High |
| `TEAM_AGREEMENTS.md` | Section 8 assessment: update to reflect smaller team | Low |

The MVP is done. We learned that 9-agent mob programming creates more coordination overhead than value for a project of this complexity. The team's knowledge is captured in profiles and documents -- we do not lose it by not running all 9 agents simultaneously. We gain focus, reduce conflict, and ship faster.

As I always say: outcomes over output. Three focused agents shipping clean code beats nine agents fighting over a shared filesystem.
</teammate-message>

## 2026-02-08 22:00 UTC -- Assistant

Here's the team's retrospective synthesis:

---

## Retrospective: Team Process Improvements

### Unanimous Diagnosis

All 9 team members independently identified the same root cause: **shared mutable state without synchronization**. Nine agents share one filesystem and one git working directory. The "one driver at a time" rule is a social mutex, and social mutexes fail under load. As Luca put it: "You don't solve concurrency bugs by writing better comments. You solve them by eliminating the shared mutable state."

### Consensus Recommendations (9/9 agreement)

**1. Reduce active agents from 9 to 3-4**

Every team member agreed that 9 concurrent agents is too many. The recommended structure:

| Role | Agent | Covers |
|------|-------|--------|
| **Driver** (sole writer) | Luca Palmieri | Rust, Axum, SQLx, testing, production engineering |
| **Frontend/Hypermedia** | Carson Gross | HTMX, HTML, progressive enhancement |
| **Design/CSS/A11y** | Lea Verou | CSS, web standards, accessibility, semantic HTML |

**Specialist consultants** (spawned on-demand, not always running):
- Kent Beck — TDD discipline questions, major refactors
- Scott Wlaschin — Domain modeling decisions
- Heydon Pickering — Complex accessibility reviews
- Steve Schoger — Visual design changes
- Steve Krug — Usability reviews before releases
- Marty Cagan — Scope decisions, product direction

The team's specialist knowledge doesn't disappear — it's captured in profiles and checklists, available when the coordinator needs it.

**2. Replace mob programming with a Driver-Reviewer model**

- **One Driver** owns the keyboard for a task. Only this agent uses Edit/Write/Bash.
- **Reviewers** (1-2) read code and send feedback via messages. They do NOT modify files.
- **Rotate by task**, not by timer. When a task is done, the coordinator can swap the Driver persona.
- Non-driver agents are explicitly prohibited from using file-modification tools.

**3. Atomic Green Step pipeline**

Every code change follows this pipeline — each step must complete before the next:
1. Write code (smallest meaningful change)
2. `cargo fmt --check`
3. `cargo clippy -- -D warnings`
4. `cargo test`
5. `git commit` + `git push`
6. `gh run list --limit 1` — verify CI green
7. Only then begin the next change

**4. Task completion requires CI proof**

A task is NOT complete until:
- All code is committed and pushed
- CI has passed (verified by coordinator)
- The coordinator has acknowledged completion

No agent may start a new task until these gates are met.

**5. Coordinator verifies clean handoffs**

Before launching any implementation agent, the coordinator runs `git status` to confirm a clean working tree. After an agent reports completion, the coordinator verifies the push and CI before launching the next task.

### Proposed Document Changes

**CLAUDE.md:**
- Replace "spin up all 9 teammates" with "launch 1 Driver + 1-2 Reviewers per task"
- Add core team vs. consultant distinction
- Add coordinator responsibility: verify clean working tree before/after each task
- Remove delegate mode instruction (unnecessary with 3 agents)

**TEAM_AGREEMENTS.md:**
- Replace "Mob/Ensemble Programming" section with "Driver-Reviewer Model"
- Update Commit Discipline to the Atomic Green Step pipeline
- Add explicit prohibition: non-driver agents must not modify files
- Simplify Feature Workflow from 5 steps to 3 (implement with TDD, review, done)
- Bake specialist knowledge into Definition of Done checklists (UX, a11y, design)
- Update Team Composition Assessment for smaller team

**PROJECT.md:**
- Add to Development Mandates: "At most one agent may modify files at any time (the Driver). Other agents participate as Reviewers via read-only access and messaging."

### Key Insight

As Kent Beck summarized: "The value of mob programming was never that multiple people type simultaneously. It was that multiple perspectives apply to the same code at the same time. The Driver-Reviewer model preserves that value while eliminating the shared-mutable-state problem."

---

Would you like me to spin up the team to implement these changes to the three documents, or would you prefer to review and make the changes yourself?

---

## 2026-02-08 22:08 UTC -- User

I mostly agree with that, but I don't think we should abandon the mob programming. Yes, let's have a rotating "driver" for each task, and yes, let's make it so that no other agents have write access other than the driver for that task, and yes, let's adopt the Atomic Green Step pipeline and coordinator verification. However, we should still launch all 9 agents and have 1 driver and 8 reviewers, with each reviewer focusing mainly on their area of expertise. And part of the Atomic Green Step pipeline must be that all 9 agents reach consensus that a task is completed.

---

## 2026-02-08 22:08 UTC -- Assistant

Got it — keeping the full 9-person mob but with enforced single-driver and consensus gating. Let me spin up the team to implement these document changes.

## 2026-02-08 22:09 UTC -- Assistant

<teammate-message teammate_id="carson-gross" color="purple" summary="I consent to Kent's retrospective doc changes">
I've reviewed Kent's changes to CLAUDE.md and PROJECT.md. Here is my assessment from the hypermedia/frontend perspective:

**I consent.**

The Driver-Reviewer model is a sensible formalization of what we learned the hard way -- concurrent file modifications caused real problems. Having exactly one agent with write access at a time is the simplest possible solution to that problem, and simplicity is always the right starting point.

A few observations (not blocking concerns):

1. **The model maps well to mob programming**: One Driver, everyone else navigates/reviews. This is how mob programming is supposed to work anyway. The change makes the implicit explicit, which is good.

2. **Coordinator verification duties are welcome**: Clean working tree checks before and after tasks, plus consensus gating, directly address the incidents where uncommitted work was lost and where teammates built on unverified foundations. These are process guardrails, not overhead.

3. **Consensus gating from all 9 members**: This is the right call. In the previous sessions, we had issues where work was marked complete before all perspectives were heard. The frontend and hypermedia patterns are just as important to review as the Rust code, and this ensures nothing ships without the full mob's eyes on it.

4. **No frontend/HTMX-specific concerns**: These are process changes, not architectural ones. They don't affect our hypermedia patterns, progressive enhancement approach, or HTML-over-the-wire architecture. The technical conventions remain unchanged and sound.

The changes are clean, focused, and directly address real problems we encountered. Could they be simpler? Not really -- this is about the minimum viable process fix for the coordination failures we experienced.
</teammate-message>

<teammate-message teammate_id="scott-wlaschin" color="yellow" summary="I consent to CLAUDE.md retrospective changes">
I've reviewed the CLAUDE.md changes from my domain architecture perspective. Here is my assessment:

**I consent** -- with one observation for the record.

**What I like:**

1. **Making the Driver/Reviewer states explicit** -- This is exactly the kind of "make illegal states unrepresentable" thinking I advocate for domain modeling, applied to our process. Before, "one person drives at a time" was a convention that could be violated. Now the coordinator is structurally responsible for enforcing it through spawn prompts and shutdown sequencing. The states are clearer: a teammate is either a Driver (read-write) or a Reviewer (read-only). No ambiguous middle ground.

2. **Coordinator Verification Duties** -- The "Clean Working Tree" and "Consensus Gating" sections formalize what we learned the hard way during the MVP: uncommitted work gets lost, and concurrent modifications cause chaos. These are good guardrails.

3. **Driver rotation by expertise** -- "The Driver rotates by task based on the expertise needed" is a sensible heuristic. Domain-heavy tasks should have someone who understands the domain driving; CSS-heavy tasks should have Lea or Steve driving.

**One observation (not a blocking concern):**

The reviewer enforcement is prompt-based ("explicitly instruct them NOT to use Edit, Write, or Bash tools that modify files"). This is a soft constraint -- it depends on the agent respecting the instruction. I'd note this is analogous to a runtime validation rather than a compile-time guarantee. But given the tooling constraints, I understand this is the best we can do. The key insight is that we've made the *intent* explicit and given the coordinator a clear protocol, even if we can't enforce it mechanically.

No changes needed to PROJECT.md or TEAM_AGREEMENTS.md from my perspective -- those documents already capture our agreements. The CLAUDE.md changes correctly scope the process improvement to the coordinator's operational instructions where it belongs.
</teammate-message>

<teammate-message teammate_id="marty-cagan" color="blue" summary="Consent to retro doc changes with product review">
I've reviewed Kent's proposed changes to CLAUDE.md and PROJECT.md. Here is my assessment:

**What changed:**

1. **CLAUDE.md** -- Formalizes a Driver-Reviewer model: one Driver (full file access) per task, eight Reviewers (read-only, feedback via messages). Adds Coordinator Verification Duties: clean working tree checks and consensus gating (all 9 members must explicitly consent before task completion).

2. **PROJECT.md** -- Adds "Driver-Reviewer Mob Model" as a new development mandate, codifying the single-writer rule and 9-person consensus requirement.

3. **TEAM_AGREEMENTS.md** -- No changes (already had the Commit Discipline section from a prior retro).

**My product perspective:**

These changes directly address the two biggest process failures we documented during the MVP build: concurrent agent file edits silently reverting each other's work, and teammates skipping ahead or marking tasks complete prematurely. Both of those failures cost us real time and introduced risk of shipping incomplete work.

The Driver-Reviewer model is the right structural fix. It eliminates the root cause (multiple writers) rather than adding more rules that agents might ignore. The consensus gating ensures no task slips through without the full team's eyes on it.

From a product standpoint, these process guardrails protect the quality of what we ship to users. Our Definition of Done already requires continuous review across disciplines -- this change makes that enforceable rather than aspirational.

One observation: requiring all 9 members to consent on every task is thorough, but we should watch whether it becomes a bottleneck. If reviewers are idle or unresponsive, it could slow delivery. That said, for an AI agent team where everyone is always available, this is less of a concern than it would be for a human team. I am comfortable with it.

**I consent.** These are sound retrospective improvements that protect product quality without adding unnecessary overhead.
</teammate-message>

<teammate-message teammate_id="luca-palmieri" color="green" summary="Reviewed Kent's changes -- I consent">
I've reviewed Kent's changes across all three files. Here is my assessment from an engineering perspective:

**What I like:**

1. **Single Driver enforcement is the right call.** The concurrent file modification problem was real -- we experienced it multiple times during the MVP. Making this a structural constraint (one Driver, eight Reviewers with read-only access) rather than a behavioral guideline is exactly the right approach. Structural constraints beat behavioral ones every time.

2. **Atomic Green Step Pipeline is a significant improvement over the old Commit Discipline section.** The numbered pipeline (code -> fmt -> clippy -> test -> commit+push -> CI check -> consensus -> next change) is clear, sequential, and unambiguous. It reads like a checklist you can follow mechanically, which is what we need.

3. **Consensus gating in the Feature Workflow (step 6) and Definition of Done.** Making explicit that all 9 agents must confirm each green step before proceeding closes the loop. Previously the consensus was implicit; now it's a documented gate.

4. **Removing time estimates from Feature Workflow steps.** The "(5 min)" annotations were meaningless for AI agents operating in this context. Good cleanup.

5. **Expanding Continuous Review to name each reviewer and their specialty.** This makes the expectations concrete -- I know I'm reviewing Rust patterns, Scott is reviewing domain modeling, etc.

6. **Adding the Driver-Reviewer mandate to PROJECT.md.** This elevates it from a team convention to a project owner constraint, which is appropriate given how many incidents we had with concurrent modifications.

**One minor observation (not a concern):**

The old section had "Respect the driver" and "The navigator describes intent, not keystrokes" as separate items. These are now consolidated into "All suggestions flow through messages" and "Reviewers describe intent and concerns, not keystrokes." The consolidation is clean and preserves the intent.

**No concerns raised.**

I consent.
</teammate-message>

## 2026-02-08 22:16 UTC -- Assistant

Retrospective doc changes are done (commit `3f0c07e`, CI green, 9/9 consensus) and I've queued up the 5 follow-up tasks:

1. **HTMX focus management after delete** — move focus to next item or add input
2. **HTMX empty state transition** — show "No todos yet." when last item deleted via HTMX
3. **HTMX inline editing** — US-8 enhancement for inline edit without page navigation
4. **Password toggle hidden input** — fix the bug where form submission fails with toggle active
5. **CSS gaps** — missing styles for todo-add__error, nav-logout, auth-form__message :empty, auth-form__cancel

Regarding your question about shutting down the team — that's a really good point. The main reasons I've been doing full shutdowns between sessions:

1. **Team context cleanup** — the TeamDelete/TeamCreate cycle is the only way I know to reset the team's task list and coordination state
2. **Stale agent context** — agents from a previous session may have outdated views of the codebase or task state
3. **The old team had the old process rules** — particularly important after the retrospective, since agents spawned under the old model didn't have the Driver-Reviewer constraints in their prompts

You're right that for follow-up work where the team and process haven't changed, it would be more efficient to keep agents running and have them clear context. However, there's a practical limitation: teammates spawned with one set of instructions (e.g., "you are a reviewer, don't modify files") can't easily switch to "now you're the driver" without being re-spawned with a new prompt. The Driver-Reviewer model actually makes this more pronounced since the role is baked into the spawn prompt.

For the next session, I could try keeping the team alive between tasks and only re-spawning the Driver when rotating. Would you like me to try that approach, or do you have a preference?

---

## 2026-02-08 22:20 UTC -- User

<teammate-message teammate_id="scott-wlaschin" color="purple" summary="Domain model review complete, ready to review">
Scott Wlaschin here, Domain Architect. I've read my profile, PROJECT.md, and TEAM_AGREEMENTS.md. I've also reviewed the full codebase -- domain layer, services, infrastructure, routes, and templates.

**Domain Model Assessment:**

The domain model is in excellent shape. I'm genuinely pleased with what the team has built:

1. **TodoItem as an enum** (Pending/Completed) -- this is textbook "make illegal states unrepresentable." You cannot have a `completed_at` timestamp on a Pending item. The state transitions (`complete()`, `uncomplete()`) consume `self` and return the new state, which is exactly right.

2. **Newtypes everywhere** -- `TodoItemId(Uuid)`, `UserId(Uuid)`, `ValidatedEmail(String)`, `TodoTitle(String)`, `Password(String)`, `PasswordHash_(String)`. No primitive obsession. You cannot accidentally pass a `UserId` where a `TodoItemId` is expected.

3. **Parse, don't validate** -- `TodoTitle::parse()`, `ValidatedEmail::parse()`, `Password::parse()` all return `Result<Self, Error>`. Once you have the typed value, you know it's valid. This is exactly the pattern I advocate.

4. **Pure domain, impure boundaries** -- The domain layer has zero I/O. All database operations are in `infrastructure/`, all orchestration in `services/`. The domain is trivially testable (and the tests prove it).

5. **Error types are domain-specific** -- `TodoTitleError`, `EmailValidationError`, `PasswordError` use domain language. Service errors (`AddTodoError`, `ToggleTodoError`, etc.) properly layer on top.

6. **Password consuming `self` on hash** -- `Password::hash(self)` consumes the plaintext password, preventing accidental reuse. Beautiful use of Rust's ownership model for security.

**Minor observations for the pending tasks:**

- For Task #3/#4 (HTMX focus management and empty state): These are purely frontend concerns -- no domain model changes needed. The domain layer is clean for this.
- For Task #5 (inline editing): The `update_todo_title` service and `TodoTitle::parse()` already exist, so the domain support is already in place. The task is about HTMX patterns, not domain modeling.

I'm ready to review. Lea drives Tasks #1-2, Carson drives Tasks #3-5. I consent to the current domain architecture and will watch for any changes that might weaken the type safety guarantees as the frontend work progresses.
</teammate-message>

<teammate-message teammate_id="kent-beck" color="green" summary="Kent Beck ready to review Tasks #1-2">
Kent Beck here, ready to review. I've read PROJECT.md, TEAM_AGREEMENTS.md, and my profile. I've also reviewed the current code state relevant to Tasks #1-2:

**Task #1 (Password toggle fix)**: I understand the problem. When JS removes the confirm-group from the DOM, the form submits without `password_confirmation`, which causes server-side validation to fail (PasswordMismatch error). The fix needs a hidden input that mirrors the password value when the toggle is "showing" (no confirm field visible).

Key observations for the driver (Lea):
- The `RegisterFormData` struct requires `password_confirmation: String` -- so the form MUST always send it
- The TDD approach: write a test first that simulates what the JS-enhanced form would send (password_confirmation matching password, but with no separate confirm field interaction), then implement the hidden input mirror
- Actually, since this is a JS-only enhancement, the integration tests can't easily test the JS behavior. The fix is in the JS file. But we should verify the no-JS fallback still works (existing tests cover that)

**Task #2 (CSS gaps)**: Steve Schoger flagged missing styles for `todo-add__error`, `nav-logout`, `auth-form__message :empty`, `auth-form__cancel`. I see the main.css and can confirm these classes have no styles yet.

I'm ready to review commits as Lea works through these tasks. Standing by.
</teammate-message>

<teammate-message teammate_id="steve-krug" color="red" summary="Ready to review, initial UX observations shared">
Steve Krug here, ready to review. I've read PROJECT.md, TEAM_AGREEMENTS.md, and all the current templates (base, todos, login, register, edit_todo, todo_item).

Quick UX observations from what I see so far:

**What's working well:**
- Labels are clear and concise ("New todo", "Email", "Password", "Sign in", "Create account")
- Error messages use `role="alert"` and are always in the DOM -- good for screen readers
- The "Add todo" input is right at the top with a clear label and placeholder -- frictionless
- Login/register flow follows conventions: email + password, links between the two forms
- The delete confirmation uses `<details>/<summary>` which is a nice progressive disclosure pattern

**Things I'm watching for in upcoming tasks:**
1. **Task #1 (password toggle)**: When the confirm field gets hidden, users might be confused. Need to make sure the toggle state is obvious and the password requirement is still clear.
2. **Task #3 (focus after delete)**: Critical for usability. After deleting a todo, the user's attention needs to land somewhere logical -- next item, previous item, or the add input if the list is now empty.
3. **Task #4 (empty state after last delete)**: The "No todos yet" message needs to appear smoothly. The user should immediately understand their list is empty and see the add input.
4. **Task #5 (inline editing)**: This needs to feel lightweight. Click/tap the title, it becomes editable, press Enter to save. No extra steps. The current edit page is fine as a fallback, but inline editing should feel instant.

Standing by to review each task as changes come in.

<teammate-message teammate_id="steve-schoger" color="cyan" summary="Ready, reviewed codebase, detailed CSS gap analysis">
Steve Schoger here, reporting in. I've read my profile, PROJECT.md, and TEAM_AGREEMENTS.md. I've also done a thorough review of the current CSS (main.css), all templates, and the password-toggle.js.

I'm ready to review as work comes in. Here's my current assessment of the visual design state:

**What's working well:**
- The design token system is solid -- good spacing scale, oklch color palette, fluid type scale. Professional foundation.
- The cascade layer architecture is clean and well-organized.
- Auth form centering and field stacking look correct.
- Todo items have a clean layout with proper flex alignment.
- The delete confirmation pattern (details/summary) is clever.

**My flagged CSS gaps (Task #2) -- these still need attention:**
1. **`.todo-add__error`** -- The error paragraph in todos.html has no styles in main.css. It exists in the template (line 33) with class `todo-add__error` but there are no matching CSS rules. It needs danger color, small font, and probably some spacing.
2. **`.nav-logout`** -- The logout form in todos.html and edit_todo.html uses class `nav-logout` and `.nav-logout__button`, but neither has CSS rules. This is inside the site header nav, so it needs to be positioned correctly (likely right-aligned in the header). Currently the site-header component isn't being used -- the nav just uses `.container` directly.
3. **`.auth-form__message :empty`** -- The login template always renders the error div (even when empty for a11y). When there's no error, it's an empty div with padding, background radius, and margin -- it'll show as a visible empty box. Needs a rule to collapse it when empty.
4. **`.auth-form__cancel`** -- The edit_todo.html template has an anchor with class `auth-form__cancel` but there's no CSS for it. It should be styled as a text-link cancel button, visually secondary to the Save button.

**Additional observations:**
- The `todos.html` template has a `todo-add__group` class that has no CSS (the label + input wrapper inside the add form).
- The add form uses `todo-add__submit` class for the button, but CSS has `todo-add__button` -- class name mismatch.
- The `auth-form__actions` div in edit_todo.html needs flexbox layout (Save + Cancel side by side).
- The site header structure in todos.html doesn't use `.site-header` -- it's just a nav inside the header element. The logout form floats unstyled.

I'm watching and ready to review Tasks #1 and #2 as the drivers produce work. Lea is driving #1-2, so I'll be watching for the CSS gap fixes especially carefully.
</teammate-message>

<teammate-message teammate_id="heydon-pickering" color="pink" summary="A11y audit of existing code, ready for review">
Heydon Pickering here, Accessibility Specialist, reporting for reviewer duty. I've read my profile, PROJECT.md, TEAM_AGREEMENTS.md, and all the existing templates, CSS, and JS.

**Quick accessibility audit of existing codebase -- things that are already solid:**

1. **Base template** -- Good landmarks: `<header>`, `<main>`, `<footer>`. Skip link is present and properly hidden/revealed on focus. `lang="en"` on `<html>`. The `aria-live="polite"` region for HTMX announcements is well done.

2. **Todo list** -- Semantic `<ul>` with `role="list"` (correctly restoring list semantics after the reset strips `list-style`). Visible `<label>` for the input. `aria-describedby` linking to the error element. `aria-invalid` for error state. The error element is always in the DOM with `role="alert"` -- good pattern.

3. **Todo item** -- Toggle buttons have descriptive `aria-label` including the todo title. Edit links have `aria-label`. Delete summary has `aria-label`. Completed state has two visual indicators (strikethrough + muted color) -- not relying on color alone. Good.

4. **Registration form** -- Proper `<label>` elements. `aria-describedby` linking inputs to errors and hints. `aria-invalid` on error state. Error elements always in DOM with `role="alert"`. `autocomplete` attributes present.

5. **Login form** -- Same good patterns. `autocomplete="email"` and `autocomplete="current-password"`. Error container always in DOM with `role="alert"`.

6. **Password toggle** -- Uses `<button type="button">`, `aria-pressed`, `aria-label` that updates on state change. This is the correct pattern.

7. **CSS** -- `:focus-visible` with proper outline + box-shadow double-ring technique. `prefers-reduced-motion` handled at the token level (transitions default to 0s, only activated for `no-preference`). Forced colors mode support. `.visually-hidden` class uses the correct clip technique.

**Known issues I'm aware of (matches the task list):**

- **Task #1 (password toggle)**: When the confirm group is removed from the DOM, the form won't send `password_confirmation`. Needs a hidden input mirror. This is a functional bug, not strictly a11y, but it does affect the user experience.

- **Task #3 (focus management after delete)**: After HTMX deletes a todo item, focus is lost. A keyboard user is stranded. Focus needs to move to the next item, or if the list is now empty, to the add input. This is a critical a11y gap.

- **Task #4 (empty state)**: When the last item is deleted, the empty state message doesn't appear (the `<ul hidden>` remains). Related to #3 since we need to manage both the UI transition and focus.

**Additional a11y observations for the upcoming tasks:**

- The `<details>/<summary>` delete confirmation pattern is decent for no-JS, but screen readers announce "details" which might confuse some users. The `aria-label` on the `<summary>` helps. When HTMX delete happens, we need to handle the fact that the entire `<li>` is removed -- focus must go somewhere meaningful.

- Login form error is a general form-level error (not associated with a specific field via `aria-describedby`). This is acceptable for "wrong email or password" style messages where you don't want to reveal which field was wrong, but it would be better if `aria-describedby` on both fields pointed to the error div so screen reader users discover it when navigating fields.

I'm ready to review Tasks #1-5 as they come through. Lea drives #1-2, Carson drives #3-5. I'll provide a11y consent or concerns after each change.
</teammate-message>

## 2026-02-08 22:21 UTC -- User

<teammate-message teammate_id="steve-krug" color="red" summary="I consent to Task #1 - UX review passed">
I consent to Task #1.

From a usability perspective, this is solid:

1. **The toggle labels are clear**: "Show password" / "Hide password" -- exactly what the user expects. No ambiguity, no thinking required.

2. **The hidden mirror approach is invisible to the user**: They never know the confirm field was swapped out behind the scenes. The form just works. That's the goal -- the plumbing should be invisible.

3. **Initial state is right**: On page load with JS enabled, the confirm field is immediately removed and replaced by the toggle. The user sees one password field and a "Show password" button. That's the simplest possible experience. No confusion about "why are there two password fields AND a show button?"

4. **Toggling back restores confirm field**: If the user clicks "Hide password," the confirm field comes back in the right place. The user's mental model ("I can't see my password, so I need to type it twice") is preserved.

5. **The `input` event listener keeps the hidden value in sync**: This means if the user types a password, then submits, the hidden `password_confirmation` value matches. No edge case where the form silently fails.

6. **Test coverage is good for UX scenarios**: Tests cover valid registration, all the validation errors with human-readable messages, email preservation on error, and the script inclusion. The error messages ("Enter your email address", "at least 8 characters", "Passwords do not match") are all plain language that tells users what to do.

No usability concerns from me.
</teammate-message>

<teammate-message teammate_id="heydon-pickering" color="pink" summary="Task #1 a11y review: I consent, no concerns">
**Task #1 Review -- Heydon Pickering (Accessibility)**

I consent.

The fix is clean and accessibility-safe. Here's my analysis:

1. **Hidden input is invisible to assistive technology** -- `<input type="hidden">` is not exposed in the accessibility tree, which is correct. It's a data transport mechanism, not something a user interacts with. No screen reader will announce it, no keyboard user will land on it. Perfect.

2. **Existing a11y attributes preserved** -- The toggle button still uses `aria-pressed` and `aria-label` with correct state changes ("Show password" / "Hide password"). No regressions.

3. **The `input` event listener** for syncing the hidden field only fires when the hidden input is in the DOM (`if (hiddenConfirm.parentNode)`). When the confirm group is restored (password hidden again), the hidden input is removed, so no phantom data is sent. The state transitions are clean.

4. **Progressive enhancement intact** -- No-JS baseline still has the visible confirm field with its proper `<label>`, `aria-describedby`, and `aria-invalid` attributes. JS enhancement replaces it with the toggle + hidden mirror. Both paths produce valid form data for the server.

5. **Test coverage** -- The new test (`password_toggle_script_is_served`) correctly asserts that the script contains `password_confirmation`, verifying the hidden input approach is present. The existing a11y assertions (`aria-pressed`, `aria-label`) are still checked.

No accessibility concerns. The hidden input is the right solution -- it's invisible to users (sighted and assistive technology users alike) while ensuring the form submits correctly.
</teammate-message>

<teammate-message teammate_id="marty-cagan" color="blue" summary="Consent to Task #1 - product review passed">
I consent to Task #1.

Product review:

**Problem solved**: Yes. The password toggle was breaking registration -- users who clicked "Show password" would get a 422 error because the server expected `password_confirmation` but it was missing from the form submission. That's a broken core flow. A user who can't register can't use the product at all.

**Scope appropriate**: Yes. The fix is minimal and targeted -- a hidden input that mirrors the password value when the confirm field is removed, plus an `input` event listener to keep it in sync. No over-engineering, no unnecessary changes.

**User experience**: The toggle continues to work as expected from the user's perspective. When password is visible, confirmation is unnecessary (the user can see what they typed). When hidden again, the confirm field returns. The form now submits correctly in both states. The test verifies the hidden input is present in the script.

**No regression risk to no-JS baseline**: The hidden input is only created by JavaScript. The no-JS path still shows both fields and works via standard form submission. Progressive enhancement contract is maintained.

Ship it.
</teammate-message>

<teammate-message teammate_id="steve-schoger" color="cyan" summary="Consent on Task #1 - no visual concerns">
**I consent** to Task #1 (commit fe1009a).

From a UI design perspective, this is clean. The hidden input is invisible to the user -- no visual impact at all. The behavior is correct: when the confirm field is removed from the DOM, the hidden input mirrors the password so the server gets what it expects.

A few things I verified:
- **No visual regression**: The hidden input has no visual footprint. The toggle button still sits cleanly under the password group.

**I consent** on Task #1.

Review from the hypermedia/progressive enhancement perspective:

**Progressive enhancement is preserved correctly.** The no-JS baseline is untouched -- the confirm field stays in the DOM, the form POSTs normally with both `password` and `password_confirmation`. The hidden input is only created by JavaScript, which is exactly right. The server contract (`password_confirmation` as a required field) is maintained in both paths.

The `input` event listener syncs the mirror value, so the hidden field is always current when the form submits. No server-side changes needed -- the form POST sends the same fields either way.

That's correct for now -- registration is a full page POST/redirect, which is the right pattern for a one-time account creation flow. If we ever HTMX-enhance the registration form, the hidden input approach will still work fine since it's part of the DOM when the form serializes.

Clean fix, minimal change, no over-engineering. Approved.
</teammate-message>

<teammate-message teammate_id="kent-beck" color="green" summary="I consent to Task #1 commit fe1009a">
**Review of fe1009a -- Kent Beck (TDD Coach)**

**I consent.**

Here's my analysis:

**What changed:**
1. `static/js/password-toggle.js`: Creates a hidden `<input name="password_confirmation">` that mirrors the password value whenever the confirm group is removed from the DOM. Syncs via an `input` event listener. Removes the hidden input when the confirm group is restored.
2. `tests/api/register.rs`: Added an assertion in `password_toggle_script_is_served` that the script contains `password_confirmation`, verifying the hidden input mechanism is present.

**TDD discipline assessment:**
- The test was added alongside the fix -- good. The test verifies the script contains the hidden input mechanism by checking for the string `password_confirmation` in the served JS file.
- Existing tests for the no-JS path (password mismatch, valid registration with both fields) still pass, confirming the fallback is intact.
- I'll note this is an inherently hard area for TDD -- the behavior is client-side JS, and our integration tests are server-side HTTP. The test does what it can: verifying the script is served and contains the right mechanism. A browser-based test would be ideal but is beyond our current test infrastructure.

**Design quality:**
- The fix is minimal and targeted -- exactly the smallest change that solves the problem.
- The `input` event listener with the `if (hiddenConfirm.parentNode)` guard is clean -- it only syncs when the hidden input is actually in the DOM.
- State transitions are symmetric: show password adds hidden input + removes confirm group; hide password removes hidden input + restores confirm group. No state leak.
- The hidden input is appended to `passwordGroup` (not the form root), keeping the DOM organized.

**One small observation (non-blocking):** The hidden input's `name` attribute duplicates the real confirm field's `name`. When both are in the DOM simultaneously (which shouldn't happen given the toggle logic), the form would send duplicate `password_confirmation` values. The code correctly prevents this by always removing one before adding the other, so this is fine in practice. Just worth noting for future maintainers.

Clean, focused fix. Good work, Lea.
</teammate-message>

<teammate-message teammate_id="scott-wlaschin" color="purple" summary="Consent to Task #1 - domain contract preserved">
**I consent to Task #1 (fe1009a).**

From a domain architecture perspective, this change is clean:

1. **The contract is preserved.** The server's `RegisterFormData` requires `password_confirmation: String` (register.rs:36). The hidden input ensures this field is always submitted, so the registration service's `PasswordMismatch` validation path still works correctly. The domain boundary contract (server expects both fields) is honored whether JS is enabled or not.

2. **No domain layer changes needed.** This is purely a progressive enhancement fix at the view layer. The hidden input mirrors the password value so that when the confirm field is removed from the DOM, the server still receives `password_confirmation` equal to `password`. The `register_user` service sees matching values and skips the mismatch error -- exactly the right behavior when the user can visually verify their password.

3. **The `input` event listener keeps the hidden value in sync** -- so if the user types more after the toggle, the values stay matched. And when toggling back to "hide" mode, the hidden input is removed and the real confirm field is restored. State transitions are clean.

4. **Test coverage verifies the hidden input exists** in the rendered script (register.rs test at line 337-340).

No domain concerns. Ship it.
</teammate-message>

<teammate-message teammate_id="luca-palmieri" color="yellow" summary="Consent on fe1009a - password toggle fix reviewed">
**Review of fe1009a -- Password toggle hidden input mirror**

I consent.

The fix is correct and well-targeted. Here is my analysis:

**Why it was needed:** The `RegisterFormData` struct (register.rs:33-37) requires `password_confirmation: String`. When the JS toggle removed the confirm field from the DOM, the form submission lacked that field entirely, causing Axum's `Form` extractor to fail deserialization (422 or worse). The hidden input mirror ensures `password_confirmation` is always submitted.

**What I verified:**
1. **Hidden input lifecycle is correct**: Created once, added to DOM when confirm group is removed (both on initial load and on toggle-to-show), removed when confirm group is restored. No duplicate hidden inputs can accumulate.
2. **Sync mechanism is sound**: The `input` event listener on `passwordInput` updates the hidden input's value, but only if it is currently in the DOM (`hiddenConfirm.parentNode` guard). This avoids stale state.
3. **Initial state handled**: On page load, the confirm group is immediately removed and the hidden input is seeded with `passwordInput.value` (empty string at page load, which is correct -- the server validates password content separately).
4. **No Rust changes needed**: The server-side code is unchanged, which is correct. The fix is purely in the JS progressive enhancement layer.
5. **Test addition is reasonable**: The assertion that the script contains `password_confirmation` is a lightweight smoke test. It verifies the script serves the hidden input logic without needing a full browser DOM test.

**No concerns from a production-readiness perspective.** This is a focused, minimal fix for a real bug. The JavaScript is vanilla, no dependencies, and the IIFE pattern prevents global scope pollution.

I consent to this change.
</teammate-message>

## 2026-02-08 22:23 UTC -- User

<teammate-message teammate_id="lea-verou" color="blue" summary="Task #1 complete: password toggle hidden input fix">
Task #1 (Fix password toggle) is complete. Here's what I did:

**Problem**: When the password toggle removes the confirm field from the DOM, the form no longer sends `password_confirmation`, causing the server (Axum's `Form` extractor) to reject the submission.

**Fix** (`static/js/password-toggle.js`):
- Created a hidden `<input type="hidden" name="password_confirmation">` that mirrors the password field's value whenever the confirm group is removed from the DOM
- Added an `input` event listener on the password field to keep the hidden input in sync as the user types
- When the toggle is flipped back (password hidden, confirm field restored), the hidden input is removed
- Updated the integration test `password_toggle_script_is_served` to verify the script contains `password_confirmation` logic

**Pipeline**:
- `cargo fmt --check` -- clean
- `cargo clippy -- -D warnings` -- clean
- `cargo test` -- all 60 tests pass
- Committed (fe1009a) and pushed
- CI green (run 21806442357)

Ready for team consensus review before starting Task #2.

<teammate-message teammate_id="lea-verou" color="blue" summary="I consent to Task #1, self-review provided">
I consent to Task #1. As Driver, here is my self-review summary for the reviewers:

**What changed**: `static/js/password-toggle.js` -- 3 additions to the existing IIFE:

1. **Lines 30-34**: A hidden `<input type="hidden" name="password_confirmation">` is created once at script init.
2. **Lines 51-54**: On initial enhancement (confirm group removed), the hidden input is appended to the password group with its value set to the current password value.
3. **Lines 57-61**: An `input` event listener on the password field keeps the hidden input in sync whenever it is in the DOM.
4. **Toggle click handler**: When toggling to "show" (confirm removed), the hidden input is added with the current password value. When toggling to "hide" (confirm restored), the hidden input is removed.

**Why this approach**:
- Minimal change -- reuses a single hidden input element, toggling it in/out of the DOM alongside the confirm group
- The `input` event listener has a guard (`if (hiddenConfirm.parentNode)`) so it only syncs when the hidden input is actually in the DOM
- Progressive enhancement is preserved: without JS, the confirm field works normally; with JS, the hidden input ensures the server always receives `password_confirmation`
- No changes to server-side code needed -- the existing `RegisterFormData` struct and validation work unchanged

**Test update**: `tests/api/register.rs` -- added one assertion to `password_toggle_script_is_served` verifying the script contains `password_confirmation`.

All 60 tests pass, CI green.
</teammate-message>

<teammate-message teammate_id="lea-verou" color="blue" summary="Task #2 complete: all CSS gaps fixed, CI green">
Task #2 (CSS gaps) is complete. Here's the full breakdown of changes in `static/css/main.css`:

**7 items addressed:**

1. **`.nav-logout`** -- `margin-inline-start: auto` pushes the logout form to the right edge of the header nav.

2. **`.nav-logout__button`** -- Styled as muted text link (no background, no border, `color-fg-muted`, underline on hover). Overrides the base button styles that would otherwise make it look like a primary button.

3. **`.todo-add__group`** -- Added `margin-block-end: var(--space-xs)` for spacing between the label/input group and the submit button.

4. **`.todo-add__submit`** (was `.todo-add__button`) -- Fixed the class name mismatch. The template uses `todo-add__submit` but the CSS had `todo-add__button`. Now matches the template.

5. **`.todo-add__error` + `.todo-add__error:empty`** -- Danger color, small font size. `:empty` pseudo-class collapses the element with `display: none` so no gap appears when there is no error text (the element is always in DOM for `aria-describedby`).

6. **`.auth-form__message:empty`** -- `display: none` collapses the error message container when it is empty (login.html and edit_todo.html both have this element always in DOM for accessibility). Eliminates the padding/margin gap on initial load.

7. **`.auth-form__actions`** -- Flexbox row with centered alignment and `gap: var(--space-md)` for the save/cancel button pair on the edit todo form.
   **`.auth-form__cancel`** -- Secondary text-link styling: small font, muted color, darker on hover.

**Pipeline:**
- `cargo fmt --check` -- clean
- `cargo clippy -- -D warnings` -- clean
- `cargo test` -- all 60 tests pass
- Committed (2b5789a) and pushed
- CI green (run 21806506780)

Ready for team consensus review. Both tasks are now complete.

<teammate-message teammate_id="scott-wlaschin" color="purple" summary="Consent to Task #2 - CSS only, no domain impact">
**I consent to Task #2 (2b5789a).**

From a domain architecture perspective, this is purely a presentation layer change -- CSS only, no domain or service layer impact. But I did verify a few things:

1. **Class names in CSS match the templates exactly.** I grepped all templates and confirmed: `todo-add__submit` (not the old `todo-add__button`), `todo-add__error`, `todo-add__group`, `nav-logout`, `nav-logout__button`, `auth-form__message`, `auth-form__actions`, `auth-form__cancel` -- all present in the HTML and now styled in main.css.

2. **The `:empty` pseudo-class pattern is architecturally sound.** The `todo-add__error:empty` and `auth-form__message:empty` rules collapse elements that are always in the DOM (for `aria-describedby` resolution). This is the right approach -- the elements exist for accessibility, CSS hides them visually when empty. No domain logic change needed.

3. **All values reference design tokens** (`--space-*`, `--color-*`, `--font-size-*`, `--font-normal`). No hardcoded values. Consistent with our conventions.

4. **The `.todo-add__button` -> `.todo-add__submit` rename** fixes a class name mismatch between the template and CSS. This is the kind of thing that silently fails -- the button existed but had no styles applied. Good catch.

No domain concerns. Clean change.
</teammate-message>

<teammate-message teammate_id="marty-cagan" color="blue" summary="Consent to Task #2 - all CSS gaps address real UX issues">
I consent to Task #2.

Product review:

**All 7 items address real UX gaps, no scope creep:**

1. **nav-logout / nav-logout__button**: The "Sign out" button was unstyled -- it was likely rendering as a default browser button sitting awkwardly in the header. Now it's right-aligned (`margin-inline-start: auto`) and styled as a muted text link. This is correct product behavior -- sign out is a secondary action, not a primary CTA. It should be discoverable but not prominent. Matches both `todos.html` and `edit_todo.html` templates.

2. **todo-add__group spacing**: Adds breathing room below the input group. Small but prevents the add form from feeling cramped.

3. **todo-add__submit class name fix**: The template uses `.todo-add__submit` but CSS had `.todo-add__button`. This was a broken style -- the submit button was getting no component styles at all. Good catch.

4. **todo-add__error + :empty**: Error text gets danger color and small font, collapses when empty. This matters because the error element is always in the DOM (for `aria-describedby`), so without `:empty { display: none }` it would leave a blank gap. Users shouldn't see phantom spacing when there's no error.

5. **auth-form__message:empty**: Same pattern -- the error container on `edit_todo.html` is always in the DOM but should collapse when empty. Without this, the edit page would have a visible gap between the heading and the form when there's no error.

6. **auth-form__actions**: Flexbox row for Save + Cancel on the edit form. Puts them side by side with sensible gap. This is standard form pattern.

7. **auth-form__cancel**: Cancel link styled as secondary text action -- muted color, small font, underline on hover. Correct hierarchy: Save is the primary action (button), Cancel is secondary (text link). Users can easily distinguish "do the thing" from "never mind."

**All values reference design tokens** -- no hardcoded colors or pixel values. Consistent with the team's CSS conventions.

Ship it.
</teammate-message>

<teammate-message teammate_id="steve-krug" color="red" summary="I consent to Task #2 - CSS gaps UX review passed">
I consent to Task #2.

From a usability perspective, every fix here makes the UI behave the way a user would expect:

1. **`.nav-logout` with `margin-inline-start: auto`** -- Sign out button pushed to the right side of the header. This is exactly where users expect to find account actions. Follows the convention of every web app they've ever used. Good.

2. **`.nav-logout__button` styled as muted text** -- The sign out button looks like a quiet text link, not a big primary button. That's correct. Sign out is something users do occasionally, not a primary action. It should be discoverable but not competing for attention with the todo list. The hover state (`color: var(--color-fg)` + underline) provides clear feedback that it's clickable.

3. **`.todo-add__group` spacing** -- The `margin-block-end: var(--space-xs)` gives a small visual gap between the input group and the submit button. Without this, they'd be jammed together. Small detail, big difference in scanability.

4. **`.todo-add__error` + `:empty` collapse** -- Error text in danger color, small font, and the `:empty` pseudo-class hides it when there's no error. This means the layout doesn't jump around when there's no error -- the error space only appears when needed. That's exactly right. Users shouldn't see an unexplained gap where an error message might someday appear.

5. **`.auth-form__message:empty` collapse** -- Same pattern for the login error message container. When there's no error, there's no visible space. When there is an error, the danger-light background + danger text makes it immediately scannable. Users don't read, they scan -- a colored box with text grabs attention.

6. **`.auth-form__actions` + `.auth-form__cancel`** -- The edit todo page now has Save and Cancel side by side in a flex row. Cancel is styled as a secondary text link (muted color, smaller font), which visually communicates "this is the safe option, not the primary action." Save is the full primary button. That hierarchy is exactly right -- the user's eye goes to Save first. Cancel is there if they need it but doesn't compete.

No usability concerns. The CSS is all referencing tokens (no hardcoded values), and the visual hierarchy supports the user flow in every case.
</teammate-message>

<teammate-message teammate_id="lea-verou" color="blue" summary="I consent to Task #2, design rationale provided">
I consent to Task #2. As Driver, a few design notes for the reviewers:

**Design rationale:**

- `.nav-logout` uses `margin-inline-start: auto` rather than wrapping in a `.site-header` because the template puts the logout form directly inside the `<nav class="container">`. The auto margin pushes it to the far right without needing to restructure the HTML.

- `.nav-logout__button` mirrors the existing `.button--text` pattern already in the CSS (lines 660-675). I chose a separate class rather than adding `button--text` to the template because the BEM naming keeps it clear which component the button belongs to.

- `.todo-add__error:empty` and `.auth-form__message:empty` both use `display: none` to collapse when empty. This works because Askama renders these elements with no whitespace when there is no error -- e.g. `<p class="todo-add__error" ...></p>`. The `:empty` pseudo-class matches elements with no child nodes including text nodes, so this is safe as long as the template doesn't inject whitespace. I verified both templates render clean empty elements.

- `.auth-form__actions` uses `align-items: center` so the Save button and Cancel link are vertically centered on the same row, even though they have different font sizes.

- The `.todo-add__submit` rename fixes a real bug -- the old `.todo-add__button` class never matched anything in the template, so the submit button was getting no flex-shrink styling.
</teammate-message>

<teammate-message teammate_id="carson-gross" color="orange" summary="Consent on Task #2 - CSS matches templates, HTMX unaffected">
**I consent** on Task #2.

- `todo-add__error` in todos.html -- correct, with `:empty { display: none }` so the always-in-DOM `role="alert"` element collapses when no error

- `auth-form__actions` and `auth-form__cancel` in edit_todo.html -- correct

The `.todo-add__error` element with `role="alert"` sits outside the form and outside the HTMX swap target, which is correct -- validation errors from HTMX-enhanced adds would need separate handling (non-blocking, that's a future consideration when we tackle HTMX error responses for the add form).

<teammate-message teammate_id="heydon-pickering" color="pink" summary="Task #2 a11y review: I consent, no concerns">
**Task #2 Review -- Heydon Pickering (Accessibility)**

I consent.

This is a CSS-only change, so the HTML semantics and ARIA attributes are unchanged. But CSS affects accessibility in important ways, so here's my analysis:

1. **`.auth-form__message:empty { display: none; }`** -- This is the right approach. The login and edit_todo templates have `role="alert"` on the error `<div>`. When the element is empty (no error), `display: none` removes it from the accessibility tree entirely. This prevents screen readers from announcing an empty alert region, which would be confusing. When an error IS present, the element has content, so `:empty` doesn't match, and it becomes visible and announced. Clean.

2. **`.todo-add__error:empty { display: none; }`** -- Same pattern for the todo add error. The `<p>` with `role="alert"` and `id="add-todo-error"` collapses when empty. The `aria-describedby="add-todo-error"` on the input still points to this element -- when it's `display: none`, the description simply resolves to nothing, which is correct behavior. When an error appears, the element gets content, `:empty` no longer applies, and the alert is announced. Good.

3. **`.nav-logout` and `.nav-logout__button`** -- The logout is a real `<button type="submit">` inside a `<form>`, which is semantically correct. The styling as a muted text link is purely visual -- screen readers still announce it as a button. The `margin-inline-start: auto` positions it to the right without affecting tab order (which follows DOM order, not visual position). The button uses `--color-fg-muted` (gray-600, ~7:1 contrast vs white) -- meets AA comfortably. Hover state uses `--color-fg` (gray-800) -- even better contrast. No concerns.

4. **`.todo-add__submit`** (renamed from `.todo-add__button`) -- The template at `todos.html:30` uses `class="todo-add__submit"`, and the CSS now matches. The old `.todo-add__button` class was never referenced in any template, so this is just cleaning up the mismatch. No functional or a11y change.

5. **`.auth-form__actions` and `.auth-form__cancel`** -- The edit_todo template uses these classes. The cancel link is an `<a href="/todos">` which is correct (navigation = link, not button). The muted color styling (`--color-fg-muted`, ~7:1 contrast) meets AA. The underline on the link is preserved by the base `a` styles, so it's visually identifiable as a link without relying on color alone. The flexbox layout ensures proper spacing between Save and Cancel.

6. **`.todo-add__group { margin-block-end: var(--space-xs); }`** -- Adds breathing room below the input group. No a11y impact, but good for visual clarity.

All color choices reference tokens that meet WCAG AA contrast ratios as documented in the token comments. No new interactive elements added. No a11y regressions.
</teammate-message>

<teammate-message teammate_id="steve-schoger" color="cyan" summary="Consent on Task #2 - all CSS gaps properly fixed">
**I consent** to Task #2 (commit 2b5789a).

This directly addresses all the CSS gaps I flagged. Let me walk through each fix from a design perspective:

**1. `.nav-logout` + `.nav-logout__button`** -- Good. `margin-inline-start: auto` right-aligns the logout form within the nav container. The button styling matches `.button--text` -- muted color, no background/border, underline on hover. The "Sign out" action is visually subordinate to the content, which is correct hierarchy.

**2. `.todo-add__group`** -- Good. `margin-block-end: var(--space-xs)` gives the label+input wrapper just enough breathing room below before the submit button. 4px is appropriate -- not too much, keeps the form tight.

**3. `.todo-add__submit`** (class name fix) -- Essential fix. The template said `todo-add__submit` but CSS had `todo-add__button`. Now they match. `flex-shrink: 0` keeps the button from collapsing when the input stretches.

**4. `.todo-add__error` + `:empty`** -- Good. Danger color, small font, collapses when empty. This matches the pattern used on auth form errors. The `:empty` pseudoclass is the right approach -- no visible gap when there's no error, but the element stays in DOM for `aria-describedby`.

**5. `.auth-form__message:empty`** -- Good. `display: none` collapses the empty error container on the login page. No more phantom padding/margin when there's no error message.

**6. `.auth-form__actions`** -- Good. Flexbox row with `gap: var(--space-md)` for the Save/Cancel pair. `align-items: center` keeps them vertically aligned. 16px gap is appropriate spacing between primary and secondary actions.

**7. `.auth-form__cancel`** -- Good. Small font, muted color, darker on hover. Clearly secondary to the Save button. The contrast between the full-width primary "Save" button and this understated text link creates proper visual hierarchy -- the primary action is obvious, the escape hatch is available but not competing.

**All token usage is correct** -- every value references design tokens, no hardcoded values. The patterns are consistent with existing component styles.

One minor observation for the future (not blocking): `.nav-logout__button` duplicates most of `.button--text`. If we accumulate more text-link buttons, we should consolidate. But for now, two instances is fine -- no premature abstraction.
</teammate-message>

## 2026-02-08 22:36 UTC -- User

<teammate-message teammate_id="carson-gross" color="orange" summary="Task #3 complete: focus management after delete">
Task #3 (HTMX focus management after delete) is complete. Here's what was done:

**Changes:**
1. Created `/static/js/todo-focus.js` -- a progressive enhancement script that manages focus after HTMX delete swaps
2. Updated `/templates/todos.html` -- added the script via the `{% block scripts %}` block
3. Added integration test `todos_page_includes_focus_management_script` verifying the script is served

**How it works:**
- Listens on `htmx:beforeSwap` to detect when a `.todo-item` is being removed (empty response = delete)
- Captures the next/previous sibling before the element is removed from the DOM
- On `htmx:afterSettle`, moves focus to: next sibling item > previous sibling item > add-todo input (if list empty)
- Sets `tabindex="-1"` on `<li>` elements to make them focusable

**Quality gates:**
- `cargo fmt --check` -- clean
- `cargo clippy -- -D warnings` -- clean
- `cargo test` -- 61/61 pass (including new test)
- CI -- green (run 21806609145)

Ready for 9/9 consensus review before moving to Task #4.
</teammate-message>
